{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01ToQbPRBqVQEJHb7HWroxLg","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"200: \\n201:     We use a biased estimator for the standard deviation, equivalent to\\n202:     `numpy.std(x, ddof=0)`. Note that the choice of `ddof` is unlikely to\\n203:     affect model performance.\\n204: \\n205:     For a comparison of the different scalers, transformers, and normalizers,\\n206:     see: :ref:`sphx_glr_auto_examples_preprocessing_plot_all_scaling.py`.\\n207: \\n208:     .. warning:: Risk of data leak\\n209: \\n210:         Do not use :func:`~sklearn.preprocessing.scale` unless you know\\n211:         what you are doing. A common mistake is to apply it to the entire data\\n212:         *before* splitting into training and test sets. This will bias the\\n213:         model evaluation because information would have leaked from the test\\n214:         set to the training set.\\n215:         In general, we recommend using\\n216:         :class:`~sklearn.preprocessing.StandardScaler` within a\\n217:         :ref:`Pipeline <pipeline>` in order to prevent most risks of data\\n218:         leaking: `pipe = make_pipeline(StandardScaler(), LogisticRegression())`.\\n219: \\n220:     Examples\\n221:     --------\\n222:     >>> from sklearn.preprocessing import scale\\n223:     >>> X = [[-2, 1, 2], [-1, 0, 1]]\\n224:     >>> scale(X, axis=0)  # scaling each column independently\\n225:     array([[-1.,  1.,  1.],\\n226:            [ 1., -1., -1.]])\\n227:     >>> scale(X, axis=1)  # scaling each row independently\\n228:     array([[-1.37,  0.39,  0.98],\\n229:            [-1.22,  0.     ,  1.22]])\\n230:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n231:     X = check_array(\\n232:         X,\\n233:         accept_sparse=\\\"csc\\\",\\n234:         copy=copy,\\n235:         ensure_2d=False,\\n236:         estimator=\\\"the scale function\\\",\\n237:         dtype=FLOAT_DTYPES,\\n238:         ensure_all_finite=\\\"allow-nan\\\",\\n239:         input_name=\\\"X\\\",\\n240:     )\\n241:     if sparse.issparse(X):\\n242:         if with_mean:\\n243:             raise ValueError(\\n244:                 \\\"Cannot center sparse matrices: pass `with_mean=False` instead\\\"\\n245:                 \\\" See docstring for motivation and alternatives.\\\"\\n246:             )\\n247:         if axis != 0:\\n248:             raise ValueError(\\n249:                 \\\"Can only scale sparse matrix on axis=0,  got axis=%d\\\" % axis\\n250:             )\\n251:         if with_std:\\n252:             _, var = mean_variance_axis(X, axis=0)\\n253:             var = _handle_zeros_in_scale(var, copy=False)\\n254:             inplace_column_scale(X, 1 / np.sqrt(var))\\n255:     else:\\n256:         X = np.asarray(X)\\n257:         if with_mean:\\n258:             mean_ = np.nanmean(X, axis)\\n259:         if with_std:\\n260:             scale_ = np.nanstd(X, axis)\\n261:         # Xr is a view on the original array that enables easy use of\\n262:         # broadcasting on the axis in which we are interested in\\n263:         Xr = np.rollaxis(X, axis)\\n264:         if with_mean:\\n265:             Xr -= mean_\\n266:             mean_1 = np.nanmean(Xr, axis=0)\\n267:             # Verify that mean_1 is 'close to zero'. If X contains very\\n268:             # large values, mean_1 can also be very large, due to a lack of\\n269:             # precision of mean_. In this case, a pre-scaling of the\\n270:             # concerned feature is efficient, for instance by its mean or\\n271:             # maximum.\\n272:             if not np.allclose(mean_1, 0):\\n273:                 warnings.warn(\\n274:                     \\\"Numerical issues were encountered \\\"\\n275:                     \\\"when centering the data \\\"\\n276:                     \\\"and might not be solved. Dataset may \\\"\\n277:                     \\\"contain too large values. You may need \\\"\\n278:                     \\\"to prescale your features.\\\"\\n279:                 )\\n280:                 Xr -= mean_1\\n281:         if with_std:\\n282:             scale_ = _handle_zeros_in_scale(scale_, copy=False)\\n283:             Xr /= scale_\\n284:             if with_mean:\\n285:                 mean_2 = np.nanmean(Xr, axis=0)\\n286:                 # If mean_2 is not 'close to zero', it comes from the fact that\\n287:                 # scale_ is very small so that mean_2 = mean_1/scale_ > 0, even\\n288:                 # if mean_1 was close to zero. The problem is thus essentially\\n289:                 # due to the lack of precision of mean_. A solution is then to\\n290:                 # subtract the mean again:\\n291:                 if not np.allclose(mean_2, 0):\\n292:                     warnings.warn(\\n293:                         \\\"Numerical issues were encountered \\\"\\n294:                         \\\"when scaling the data \\\"\\n295:                         \\\"and might not be solved. The standard \\\"\\n296:                         \\\"deviation of the data is probably \\\"\\n297:                         \\\"very close to 0. \\\"\\n298:                     )\\n299:                     Xr -= mean_2\\n300:     return X\\n301: \\n302: \\n303: class MinMaxScaler(OneToOneFeatureMixin, TransformerMixin, BaseEstimator):\\n304:     \\\"\\\"\\\"Transform features by scaling each feature to a given range.\\n305: \\n306:     This estimator scales and translates each feature individually such\\n307:     that it is in the given range on the training set, e.g. between\\n308:     zero and one.\\n309: \\n310:     The transformation is given by::\\n311: \\n312:         X_std = (X - X.min(axis=0)) / (X.max(axis=0) - X.min(axis=0))\\n313:         X_scaled = X_std * (max - min) + min\\n314: \\n315:     where min, max = feature_range.\\n316: \\n317:     This transformation is often used as an alternative to zero mean,\\n318:     unit variance scaling.\\n319: \\n320:     `MinMaxScaler` doesn't reduce the effect of outliers, but it linearly\\n321:     scales them down into a fixed range, where the largest occurring data point\\n322:     corresponds to the maximum value and the smallest one corresponds to the\\n323:     minimum value. For an example visualization, refer to :ref:`Compare\\n324:     MinMaxScaler with other scalers <plot_all_scaling_minmax_scaler_section>`.\\n325: \\n326:     Read more in the :ref:`User Guide <preprocessing_scaler>`.\\n327: \\n328:     Parameters\\n329:     ----------\\n330:     feature_range : tuple (min, max), default=(0, 1)\\n331:         Desired range of transformed data.\\n332: \\n333:     copy : bool, default=True\\n334:         Set to False to perform inplace row normalization and avoid a\\n335:         copy (if the input is already a numpy array).\\n336: \\n337:     clip : bool, default=False\\n338:         Set to True to clip transformed values of held-out data to\\n339:         provided `feature_range`.\\n340:         Since this parameter will clip values, `inverse_transform` may not\\n341:         be able to restore the original data.\\n342: \\n343:         .. note::\\n344:             Setting `clip=True` does not prevent feature drift (a distribution\\n345:             shift between training and test data). The transformed values are clipped\\n346:             to the `feature_range`, which helps avoid unintended behavior in models\\n347:             sensitive to out-of-range inputs (e.g. linear models). Use with care,\\n348:             as clipping can distort the distribution of test data.\\n349: \\n350:         .. versionadded:: 0.24\\n351: \\n352:     Attributes\\n353:     ----------\\n354:     min_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,)\\n355:         Per feature adjustment for minimum. Equivalent to\\n356:         ``min - X.min(axis=0) * self.scale_``\\n357: \\n358:     scale_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,)\\n359:         Per feature relative scaling of the data. Equivalent to\\n360:         ``(max - min) / (X.max(axis=0) - X.min(axis=0))``\\n361: \\n362:         .. versionadded:: 0.17\\n363:            *scale_* attribute.\\n364: \\n365:     data_min_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,)\\n366:         Per feature minimum seen in the data\\n367: \\n368:         .. versionadded:: 0.17\\n369:            *data_min_*\\n370: \\n371:     data_max_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,)\\n372:         Per feature maximum seen in the data\\n373: \\n374:         .. versionadded:: 0.17\\n375:            *data_max_*\\n376: \\n377:     data_range_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,)\\n378:         Per feature range ``(data_max_ - data_min_)`` seen in the data\\n379: \\n380:         .. versionadded:: 0.17\\n381:            *data_range_*\\n382: \\n383:     n_features_in_ : int\\n384:         Number of features seen during :term:`fit`.\\n385: \\n386:         .. versionadded:: 0.24\\n387: \\n388:     n_samples_seen_ : int\\n389:         The number of samples processed by the estimator.\\n390:         It will be reset on new calls to fit, but increments across\\n391:         ``partial_fit`` calls.\\n392: \\n393:     feature_names_in_ : ndarray of shape (`n_features_in_`,)\\n394:         Names of features seen during :term:`fit`. Defined only when `X`\\n395:         has feature names that are all strings.\\n396: \\n397:         .. versionadded:: 1.0\\n398: \\n399:     See Also\\n400:     --------\\n401: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/scikit-learn--cb7e82dd@72904752e88f414cbc543dac5945b6c8dd7b34fa/-/blob/sklearn/preprocessing/_data.py%3FL200-400\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"852663a2-be33-4dea-b4bb-b7a20d67374c","uuid":"f2cd971a-9638-45de-b25d-4b5ec75534d8","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"200: \\n201:     We use a biased estimator for the standard deviation, equivalent to\\n202:     `numpy.std(x, ddof=0)`. This will bias the\n685:         model evaluation because information would have leaked from the test\n686:         set to the training set.\n687:         In general, we recommend using\n688:         :class:`~sklearn.preprocessing.MinMaxScaler` within a\n689:         :ref:`Pipeline <pipeline>` in order to prevent most risks of data\n690:         leaking: `pipe = make_pipeline(MinMaxScaler(), LogisticRegression())`.\n691: \n692:     See Also\n693:     --------\n694:     MinMaxScaler : Performs scaling to a given range using the Transformer\n695:         API (e.g. as part of a preprocessing\n696:         :class:`~sklearn.pipeline.Pipeline`).\n697: \n698:     Notes\n699:     -----\n700:     For a comparison of the different scalers, transformers, and normalizers,\n701:     see: :ref:`sphx_glr_auto_examples_preprocessing_plot_all_scaling.py`.\n702: \n703:     Examples\n704:     --------\n705:     >>> from sklearn.preprocessing import minmax_scale\n706:     >>> X = [[-2, 1, 2], [-1, 0, 1]]\n707:     >>> minmax_scale(X, axis=0)  # scale each column independently\n708:     array([[0., 1., 1.],\n709:            [1., 0., 0.]])\n710:     >>> minmax_scale(X, axis=1)  # scale each row independently\n711:     array([[0.  , 0.75, 1.  ],\n712:            [0.  , 0.5 , 1.  ]])\n713:     \"\"\"\n714:     # Unlike the scaler object, this function allows 1d input.\n715:     # If copy is required, it will be done inside the scaler object.\n716:     X = check_array(\n717:         X,\n718:         copy=False,\n719:         ensure_2d=False,\n720:         dtype=FLOAT_DTYPES,\n721:         ensure_all_finite=\"allow-nan\",\n722:     )\n723:     original_ndim = X.ndim\n724: \n725:     if original_ndim == 1:\n726:         X = X.reshape(X.shape[0], 1)\n727: \n728:     s = MinMaxScaler(feature_range=feature_range, copy=copy)\n729:     if axis == 0:\n730:         X = s.fit_transform(X)\n731:     else:\n732:         X = s.fit_transform(X.T).T\n733: \n734:     if original_ndim == 1:\n735:         X = X.ravel()\n736: \n737:     return X\n738: \n739: \n740: class StandardScaler(OneToOneFeatureMixin, TransformerMixin, BaseEstimator):\n741:     \"\"\"Standardize features by removing the mean and scaling to unit variance.\n742: \n743:     The standard score of a sample `x` is calculated as:\n744: \n745:     .. code-block:: text\n746: \n747:         z = (x - u) / s\n748: \n749:     where `u` is the mean of the training samples or zero if `with_mean=False`,\n750:     and `s` is the standard deviation of the training samples or one if\n751:     `with_std=False`.\n752: \n753:     Centering and scaling happen independently on each feature by computing\n754:     the relevant statistics on the samples in the training set. Mean and\n755:     standard deviation are then stored to be used on later data using\n756:     :meth:`transform`.\n757: \n758:     Standardization of a dataset is a common requirement for many\n759:     machine learning estimators: they might behave badly if the\n760:     individual features do not more or less look like standard normally\n761:     distributed data (e.g. Gaussian with 0 mean and unit variance).\n762: \n763:     For instance many elements used in the objective function of\n764:     a learning algorithm (such as the RBF kernel of Support Vector\n765:     Machines or the L1 and L2 regularizers of linear models) assume that\n766:     all features are centered around 0 and have variance in the same\n767:     order. If a feature has a variance that is orders of magnitude larger\n768:     than others, it might dominate the objective function and make the\n769:     estimator unable to learn from other features correctly as expected.\n770: \n771:     `StandardScaler` is sensitive to outliers, and the features may scale\n772:     differently from each other in the presence of outliers. For an example\n773:     visualization, refer to :ref:`Compare StandardScaler with other scalers\n774:     <plot_all_scaling_standard_scaler_section>`.\n775: \n776:     This scaler can also be applied to sparse CSR or CSC matrices by passing\n777:     `with_mean=False` to avoid breaking the sparsity structure of the data.\n778: \n779:     Read more in the :ref:`User Guide <preprocessing_scaler>`.\n780: \n781:     Parameters\n782:     ----------\n783:     copy : bool, default=True\n784:         If False, try to avoid a copy and do inplace scaling instead.\n785:         This is not guaranteed to always work inplace; e.g. if the data is\n786:         not a NumPy array or scipy.sparse CSR matrix, a copy may still be\n787:         returned.\n788: \n789:     with_mean : bool, default=True\n790:         If True, center the data before scaling.\n791:         This does not work (and will raise an exception) when attempted on\n792:         sparse matrices, because centering them entails building a dense\n793:         matrix which in common use cases is likely to be too large to fit in\n794:         memory.\n795: \n796:     with_std : bool, default=True\n797:         If True, scale the data to unit variance (or equivalently,\n798:         unit standard deviation).\n799: \n800:     Attributes\n801: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/scikit-learn--cb7e82dd@72904752e88f414cbc543dac5945b6c8dd7b34fa/-/blob/sklearn/preprocessing/_data.py%3FL600-800"}}}

Generally this is calculated using `np.sqrt(var_)`. If a\n805:         variance is zero, we can't achieve unit variance, and the data is left\n806:         as-is, giving a scaling factor of 1. `scale_` is equal to `None`\n807:         when `with_std=False`.\n808: \n809:         .. versionadded:: 0.17\n810:            *scale_*\n811: \n812:     mean_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\n813:         The mean value for each feature in the training set.\n814:         Equal to ``None`` when ``with_mean=False`` and ``with_std=False``.\n815: \n816:     var_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\n817:         The variance for each feature in the training set. If\n836:         `sample_weights` are used it will be a float (if no missing data)\n837:         or an array of dtype float that sums the weights seen so far.\n838:         Will be reset on new calls to fit, but increments across\n839:         ``partial_fit`` calls.\n840: \n841:     See Also\n842:     --------\n843:     scale : Equivalent function without the estimator API.\n844: \n845:     :class:`~sklearn.decomposition.PCA` : Further removes the linear\n846:         correlation across features with 'whiten=True'.\n847: \n848:     Notes\n849:     -----\n850:     NaNs are treated as missing values: disregarded in fit, and maintained in\n851:     transform.\n852: \n853:     We use a biased estimator for the standard deviation, equivalent to\n854:     `numpy.std(x, ddof=0)`. Note that the choice of `ddof` is unlikely to\n855:     affect model performance.\n856: \n857:     Examples\n858:     --------\n859:     >>> from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler\n860:     >>> data = [[0, 0], [0, 0], [1, 1], [1, 1]]\n861:     >>> scaler = StandardScaler()\n862:     >>> print(scaler.fit(data))\n863:     StandardScaler()\n864:     >>> print(scaler.mean_)\n865:     [0.5 0.5]\n866:     >>> print(scaler.transform(data))\n867:     [[-1. -1.]\n868:      [-1. -1.]\n869:      [ 1.  1.]\n870:      [ 1.  1.]]\n871:     >>> print(scaler.transform([[2, 2]]))\n872:     [[3. 3.]]\n873:     \"\"\"\n874: \n875:     _parameter_constraints: dict = {\n876:         \"copy\": [\"boolean\"],\n877:         \"with_mean\": [\"boolean\"],\n878:         \"with_std\": [\"boolean\"],\n879:     }\n880: \n881:     def __init__(self, *, copy=True, with_mean=True, with_std=True):\n882:         self.with_mean = with_mean\n883:         self.with_std = with_std\n884:         self.copy = copy\n885: \n886:     def _reset(self):\n887:         \"\"\"Reset internal data-dependent state of the scaler, if necessary.\n888: \n889:         __init__ parameters are not touched.\n890:         \"\"\"\n891:         # This happens because of an interaction between pandas' masked array handling and scikit-learn's input validation, causing data type mismatches that can lead to silent precision loss or errors in subsequent NumPy operations.\n\n## Root Cause\n\nThe issue stems from a chain of dtype conversion logic spanning three libraries:\n\n### 1. **Scikit-Learn Input Validation** (Primary Issue)\n**File:** `sklearn/utils/validation.py`\n**Lines:** 935-942 (in `check_array()` function)\n\n```python\nif pandas_requires_conversion:\n    # pandas dataframe requires conversion earlier to handle extension dtypes with\n    # nans\n    # Use the original dtype for conversion if dtype is None\n    new_dtype = dtype_orig if dtype is None else dtype\n    array = array.astype(new_dtype)\n    # Since we converted here, we do not need to convert again later\n    dtype = None\n```\n\nThe `pandas_requires_conversion` flag is set to `True` for nullable integer types by the `_pandas_dtype_needs_early_conversion()` function.\n\n**File:** `sklearn/utils/validation.py`\n**Lines:** 681-717 (in `_pandas_dtype_needs_early_conversion()` function)\n\n```python\nelif is_integer_dtype(pd_dtype):\n    # XXX: Warn when converting from a high integer to a float\n    return True\n```\n\nThis function returns `True` for ANY integer dtype (including pandas nullable integers like `Int64Dtype`), triggering early conversion at line 940 via `array.astype(new_dtype)`.\n\n### 2. **Pandas Masked Array Conversion** (Secondary Issue)\n**File:** `pandas/core/arrays/masked.py`\n**Lines:** 694-716 (in `__array__()` method)\n\nWhen scikit-learn calls `astype()` on a pandas nullable integer array with NA values, this invokes:\n- `__array__()` protocol (line 694-716)\n- Which calls `to_numpy()` when there are NA values (line 716)\n\n### 3. **Pandas Dtype Inference Logic** (Root Cause in Pandas)\n**File:** `pandas/core/arrays/_utils.py`\n**Lines:** 27-75 (in `to_numpy_dtype_inference()` function)\n\nThis is where the dtype promotion happens:\n\n```python\ndef to_numpy_dtype_inference(\n    arr: ExtensionArray,\n    dtype: npt.DTypeLike | None,\n    na_value,\n    hasna: bool,\n) -> tuple[np.dtype | None, Any]:\n    result_dtype: np.dtype | None\n    inferred_numeric_dtype = False\n    if dtype is None and is_numeric_dtype(arr.dtype):\n        inferred_numeric_dtype = True\n        if hasna:\n            if arr.dtype.kind == \"b\":\n                result_dtype = np.dtype(np.object_)\n            else:\n                if arr.dtype.kind in \"iu\":  # Line 41\n                    result_dtype = np.dtype(np.float64)  # Line 42 - **KEY CONVERSION**\n```\n\n**The Problem:** When a nullable integer array (kind = 'i') has NA values and no explicit dtype is specified:\n- Lines 41-42 automatically convert integer → float64\n- This is because NumPy integers cannot natively represent NA values\n- But this conversion happens silently without user awareness\n\n## Data Flow Trace\n\n```\nUser Code: StandardScaler().fit(df[['int_col']])\n    ↓\nsklearn/preprocessing/_data.py:899 - to_numpy(dtype=dtype, copy=copy)\n    ↓\npandas/core/arrays/_utils.py:27-75 - to_numpy_dtype_inference()\n    ↓ Converts Int64 with NA → float64\nnumpy/_core/_methods.py - Subsequent NumPy operations on float64 array\n```\n\n## Critical File Locations\n\n### Scikit-Learn Validation\n- **sklearn/utils/validation.py:681-717** - `_pandas_dtype_needs_early_conversion()`: Identifies nullable integers as needing early conversion\n- **sklearn/utils/validation.py:935-942** - `check_array()`: Calls `astype()` on pandas arrays flagged for early conversion\n\n### Pandas Conversion\n- **pandas/core/arrays/_utils.py:27-75** - `to_numpy_dtype_inference()`: Converts Int64 → float64 when NA values present (lines 41-42)\n- **pandas/core/arrays/masked.py:694-716** - `BaseMaskedArray.__array__()`: Protocol that delegates to `to_numpy()`\n- **pandas/core/arrays/masked.py:512-600** - `BaseMaskedArray.to_numpy()`: Calls dtype inference\n\n### NumPy Operations\n- **numpy/_core/_methods.py**: Operates on the converted float64 arrays\n\n## Symptom Examples\n\n### Example 1: Type Mismatch with Missing Values\n```python\nimport pandas as pd\nfrom Int64 → float64\nscaler = StandardScaler()\nresult = scaler.fit_transform(df)\n# result.dtype is float64, not int64!\n# With NaN values where NA was\n```\n\n### Example 2: Precision Loss\n```python\n# Very large integers lose precision in float64\ndf = pd.DataFrame({\n    'large_int': pd.array([2**53, 2**53 + 1, pd.NA], dtype='Int64')\n})\n# After conversion to float64, 2**53 and 2**53 + 1 become indistinguishable\n```\n\n### Example 3: Incompatible Dtype for Operations\n```python\n# Some operations might expect int but get float\ndf = pd.DataFrame({\n    'categorical_int': pd.array([0, 1, 2, pd.NA], dtype='Int64')\n})\n# Gets converted to [0.0, 1.0, 2.0, nan]\n# Potentially breaks categorical logic\n```\n\n## Incompatibility Chain\n\nThe core incompatibility is:\n\n```\nPandas Nullable behavior\n```\n\n## Impact\n\n1. **Silent Type Conversion:** No warning when Int64 → float64 conversion happens\n2. **Precision Loss:** Large integers (> 2^53) lose precision in float64\n3. **Semantic Mismatch:** Integer data becomes float data\n4. **Unexpected NaN Values:** NA values become np.nan in float arrays\n5. **Downstream Issues:** Other operations expecting integer data will receive float arrays\n\n## Proposed Fix Areas\n\n1. **Scikit-Learn (Primary Fix):**\n   - Modify `_pandas_dtype_needs_early_conversion()` to NOT flag nullable integers if they can be safely handled\n   - OR modify `check_array()` to pass explicit dtype information to avoid pandas' default inference\n   - OR add a warning when this conversion happens\n\n2. **Pandas (Secondary Note):**\n   - The behavior in `to_numpy_dtype_inference()` is intentional (numpy can't represent NA in int arrays)\n   - But it could provide an option to control this behavior\n\n## References\n\n- Scikit-learn Issue: Input validation performs unexpected dtype conversion for pandas nullable integers\n- Pandas Discussion: Nullable integer to numpy conversion automatically promotes to float\n- NumPy Limitation: NumPy
